<p><b>Echinoderm</b> is the common name given to any member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylum_(biology)" title="Phylum (biology)">phylum</a> <b>Echinodermata</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>, ἐχῖνος, <i>echinos</i> – "hedgehog" and δέρμα, <i>derma</i> – "skin")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-2">[2]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_(ocean)" title="Marine (ocean)">marine</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>. The adults are recognizable by their (usually five-point) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" title="Radial symmetry">radial symmetry</a>, and include such well-known animals as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_stars" title="Sea stars">sea stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sand_dollar" title="Sand dollar">sand dollars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Crinoid" title="Crinoid">sea lilies</a> or "stone lilies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-3">[3]</a></sup> Echinoderms are found at every ocean depth, from the <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_zone" title="Intertidal zone">intertidal zone</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone">abyssal zone</a>.
The phylum contains about 7000 living <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adw_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-adw-4">[4]</a></sup> making it the second-largest grouping of <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">deuterostomes</a> (a superphylum), after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">chordates</a> (which include the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>). Echinoderms are also the largest phylum that has no freshwater or terrestrial (land-based) representatives.
</p><p>Aside from the hard-to-classify <i><a href="/wiki/Arkarua" title="Arkarua">Arkarua</a></i> (a <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> animal with echinoderm-like pentamerous radial symmetry), the first definitive members of the phylum appeared near the start of the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a>. One group of Cambrian echinoderms, the cinctans (<a href="/wiki/Homalozoa" title="Homalozoa">Homalozoa</a>), which are close to the base of the echinoderm origin, have been found to possess external gills used for filter feeding, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">chordata</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemichordata" title="Hemichordata">hemichordata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The echinoderms are important both ecologically and geologically. Ecologically, there are few other groupings so abundant in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">biotic desert</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea" title="Deep sea">deep sea</a>, as well as shallower oceans. Most echinoderms are able to <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">reproduce asexually</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regenerate</a> tissue, organs, and limbs; in some cases, they can undergo complete regeneration from a single limb. Geologically, the value of echinoderms is in their <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossified</a> <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletons</a>, which are major contributors to many <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> formations, and can provide valuable clues as to the geological environment. They were the most used species in regenerative research in the 19th and 20th centuries. Further, it is held by some scientists that the radiation of echinoderms was responsible for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesozoic_Marine_Revolution" title="Mesozoic Marine Revolution">Mesozoic Marine Revolution</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Regeneration"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Regeneration</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_echinodermata_orders" title="List of echinodermata orders">List of echinodermata orders</a></div>
<p>Along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chordata" title="Chordata">chordates</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemichordata" title="Hemichordata">hemichordates</a>, echinoderms are <a href="/wiki/Deuterostome" title="Deuterostome">deuterostomes</a>, one of the two major divisions of the <a href="/wiki/Bilateria" title="Bilateria">bilaterians</a>, the other being the <a href="/wiki/Protostome" title="Protostome">protostomes</a>. During the early development of the embryo, in deuterostomes, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blastopore" title="Blastopore">blastopore</a> (the first opening to form) becomes the <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a> whereas in the protostomes, it becomes the mouth. In deuterostomes, the mouth develops at a later stage, at the opposite end of the <a href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> from the blastopore, and a gut forms connecting the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit779_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit779-6">[6]</a></sup> The larvae of echinoderms have bilateral symmetry but this is lost during <a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis" title="Metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> when their bodies are reorganised and develop the characteristic <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_biology" title="Symmetry in biology">radial symmetry</a> of the echinoderm, typically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentamerism#Pentamerism" title="Pentamerism">pentamerism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lander_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lander-7">[7]</a></sup>  The characteristics of adult echinoderms are the possession of a <a href="/wiki/Water_vascular_system" title="Water vascular system">water vascular system</a> with external <a href="/wiki/Tube_feet" title="Tube feet">tube feet</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcareous</a> endoskeleton consisting of <a href="/wiki/Ossicle_(echinoderm)" title="Ossicle (echinoderm)">ossicles</a> connected by a mesh of <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> fibres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tree_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tree-8">[8]</a></sup>
A 2014 analysis of 219 genes from all classes of echinoderms gives the following phylogenetic tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelfordLowe2014_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-TelfordLowe2014-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264556">.mw-parser-output table.clade{border-spacing:0;margin:0;font-size:100%;line-height:100%;border-collapse:separate;width:auto}.mw-parser-output table.clade table.clade{width:100%}.mw-parser-output table.clade td{border:0;padding:0;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label{width:0.8em;border:0;padding:0 0.2em;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel{border:0;padding:0 0.2em;vertical-align:top;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar{vertical-align:middle;text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf{border:0;padding:0;text-align:left;vertical-align:middle}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leafR{border:0;padding:0;text-align:right}</style>

<td class="clade-slabel"> 
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Echinosphaerites.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1548" data-file-width="1648" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Echinosphaerites.JPG/220px-Echinosphaerites.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Echinosphaerites.JPG/330px-Echinosphaerites.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Echinosphaerites.JPG/440px-Echinosphaerites.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Echinosphaerites.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ordovician cystoid <i><a href="/wiki/Echinosphaerites" title="Echinosphaerites">Echinosphaerites</a></i> from northeastern Estonia</div></div></div>
<p>There are a total of about 7,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species of echinoderm as well as about 13,000 extinct species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tree_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tree-8">[8]</a></sup> They are found in habitats ranging from shallow intertidal areas to abyssal depths. Two main subdivisions are traditionally recognised: the more familiar <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">motile</a> <a href="/wiki/Eleutherozoa" title="Eleutherozoa">Eleutherozoa</a>, which encompasses the Asteroidea (<a href="/wiki/Starfish" title="Starfish">starfish</a>, 1,745 recent species), Ophiuroidea (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle_stars" title="Brittle stars">brittle stars</a>, 2,300 species), Echinoidea (<a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sand_dollar" title="Sand dollar">sand dollars</a>, 900 species) and Holothuroidea (<a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumbers</a>, 1,430 species); and the <a href="/wiki/Pelmatozoa" title="Pelmatozoa">Pelmatozoa</a>, some of which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sessility_(zoology)" title="Sessility (zoology)">sessile</a> while others move around. These consist of the Crinoidea (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feather_star" title="Feather star">feather stars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_lily" title="Sea lily">sea lilies</a>, 580 species) and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Blastozoa" title="Blastozoa">blastoids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracrinoid" title="Paracrinoid">Paracrinoids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uthicke_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uthicke-10">[10]</a></sup> A fifth class of Eleutherozoa consisting of just three species, the Concentricycloidea (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_daisies" title="Sea daisies">sea daisies</a>), were recently merged into the Asteroidea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The fossil record includes a large number of other classes which do not appear to fall into any extant <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">crown group</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fossile-seelilie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="835" data-file-width="748" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fossile-seelilie.jpg/220px-Fossile-seelilie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fossile-seelilie.jpg/330px-Fossile-seelilie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Fossile-seelilie.jpg/440px-Fossile-seelilie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fossile-seelilie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fossil <a href="/wiki/Crinoid" title="Crinoid">crinoid</a> crowns</div></div></div>
<p>All echinoderms are <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">marine</a> and nearly all are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waggoner_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waggoner-12">[12]</a></sup> The oldest known echinoderm <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> may be <i><a href="/wiki/Arkarua" title="Arkarua">Arkarua</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> of Australia. It is a disc-like fossil with radial ridges on the rim and a five-pointed central depression marked with radial lines. However, no <a href="/wiki/Stereom" title="Stereom">stereom</a> or internal structure showing a water vascular system is present and the identification is inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first universally accepted echinoderms appear in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Cambrian" title="Lower Cambrian">Lower Cambrian</a> period, asterozoans appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Ordovician" title="Ordovician">Ordovician</a> and the crinoids were a dominant group in the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waggoner_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waggoner-12">[12]</a></sup> Echinoderms left behind an extensive fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waggoner_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Waggoner-12">[12]</a></sup> It is hypothesised that the ancestor of all echinoderms was a simple, motile, bilaterally symmetrical animal with a mouth, gut and anus. This ancestral stock adopted an attached mode of life and suspension feeding, and developed radial symmetry as this was more advantageous for such an existence. The larvae of all echinoderms are even now bilaterally symmetrical and all develop radial symmetry at metamorphosis. The starfish and crinoids still attach themselves to the seabed while changing to their adult form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit793_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit793-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first echinoderms later gave rise to free-moving groups. The evolution of endoskeletal plates with stereom structure and of external ciliary grooves for feeding were early echinoderm developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The Paleozoic echinoderms were globular, attached to the <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrate</a> and were orientated with their oral surfaces upwards. The fossil echinoderms had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambulacral_groove" title="Ambulacral groove">ambulacral grooves</a> extending down the side of the body, fringed on either side by brachioles, structures very similar to the pinnules of a modern crinoid. It seems probable that the mouth-upward orientation is the primitive state and that at some stage, all the classes of echinoderms except the crinoids reversed this to become mouth-downward. Before this happened, the podia probably had a feeding function as they do in the crinoids today. Their locomotor function came later, after the re-orientation of the mouth when the podia were in contact with the substrate for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit793_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit793-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Echinoderms evolved from animals with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)#Bilateral_symmetry" title="Symmetry (biology)">bilateral symmetry</a>. Although adult echinoderms possess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)#Animals" title="Symmetry (biology)">pentaradial</a>, or five-sided, symmetry, echinoderm larvae are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">ciliated</a>, free-swimming organisms that organize in bilateral symmetry which makes them look like embryonic <a href="/wiki/Chordate" title="Chordate">chordates</a>. Later, the left side of the body grows at the expense of the right side, which is eventually absorbed. The left side then grows in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentaradial_symmetry#Pentamerism" title="Pentaradial symmetry">pentaradially</a> symmetric fashion, in which the body is arranged in five parts around a central axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert873_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert873-16">[16]</a></sup> Within the <a href="/wiki/Asterozoa" title="Asterozoa">Asterozoa</a>, there can be a few exceptions from the rule. The starfish genus <a href="/wiki/Leptasterias" title="Leptasterias">Leptasterias</a> normally have six arms, although five armed individuals can occur. Also the <a href="/wiki/Brisingida" title="Brisingida">Brisingida</a> have six armed species. Amongst the brittle stars, six armed species such as Ophiothela danae, <a href="/wiki/Ophiactis_savignyi" title="Ophiactis savignyi">Ophiactis savignyi</a> and Ophionotus hexactis exists, and Ophiacantha vivipara often has more than six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Echinoderms exhibit secondary radial symmetry in portions of their body at some stage of life. This, however, is an adaptation to their sessile existence. They developed from other members of the Bilateria and exhibit bilateral symmetry in their larval stage. Many crinoids and some seastars exhibit symmetry in multiples of the basic five, with starfish such as <i><a href="/wiki/Labidiaster_annulatus" title="Labidiaster annulatus">Labidiaster annulatus</a></i> known to possess up to fifty arms, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea-lily" title="Sea-lily">sea-lily</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Comaster_schlegelii" title="Comaster schlegelii">Comaster schlegelii</a></i> having two hundred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messing_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Messing-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin_and_skeleton">Skin and skeleton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Skin and skeleton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reef2589.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="1796" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reef2589.jpg/220px-Reef2589.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reef2589.jpg/330px-Reef2589.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Reef2589.jpg/440px-Reef2589.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reef2589.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A brittle star, <i><a href="/wiki/Ophionereis_reticulata" title="Ophionereis reticulata">Ophionereis reticulata</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg/220px-Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg/330px-Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg/440px-Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_cucumber_at_Pulau_Redang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumber" title="Sea cucumber">sea cucumber</a> from Malaysia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nerr0878.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1236" data-file-width="1800" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nerr0878.jpg/220px-Nerr0878.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nerr0878.jpg/330px-Nerr0878.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Nerr0878.jpg/440px-Nerr0878.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nerr0878.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starfish exhibit a wide range of colours</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1374" data-file-width="1660" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg/220px-Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg/330px-Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg/440px-Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus" title="Strongylocentrotus purpuratus">Strongylocentrotus purpuratus</a></i>, a well-armoured sea urchin</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3762" data-file-width="2822" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG/220px-Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG/330px-Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG/440px-Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crinoid_on_the_reef_of_Batu_Moncho_Island.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Crinoid" title="Crinoid">Crinoid</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reef</a></div></div></div>
<p>Echinoderms have a <a href="/wiki/Mesoderm" title="Mesoderm">mesodermal</a> skeleton composed of calcareous plates or <a href="/wiki/Ossicle_(echinoderm)" title="Ossicle (echinoderm)">ossicles</a>. Each one of these, even the articulating spine of a sea urchin, is composed mineralogically of a <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>. If solid, these would form a heavy skeleton, so they have a sponge-like porous structure known as stereom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Ossicles may be fused together, as in the <a href="/wiki/Test_(biology)" title="Test (biology)">test</a> of sea urchins, or may articulate with each other as in the arms of sea stars, brittle stars and crinoids. The ossicles may be flat plates or bear external projections in the form of spines, granules or warts and they are supported by a tough <a href="/wiki/Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)">epidermis</a> (skin). Skeletal elements are also deployed in some specialized ways, such as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_lantern" title="Aristotle's lantern">Aristotle's lantern</a>" mouthparts of sea urchins used for grinding, the supportive stalks of crinoids and the structural "lime ring" of sea cucumbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert873_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert873-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the robustness of the individual skeletal modules complete skeletons of starfish, brittle stars and crinoids are rare in the fossil record. This is because they quickly disarticulate (disconnect from each other) once the encompassing skin rots away, and in the absence of tissue there is nothing to hold the plates together. The modular construction is a result of the growth system employed by echinoderms, which adds new segments at the centre of the radial limbs, pushing the existing plates outwards and lengthening the arms. Sea urchins on the other hand are often well preserved in chalk beds or limestone. During fossilization, the cavities in the stereom are filled in with calcite that is in crystalline continuity with the surrounding material. On fracturing such rock, distinctive cleavage patterns can be seen and sometimes even the intricate internal and external structure of the test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The epidermis consists of cells responsible for the support and maintenance of the skeleton, as well as pigment cells, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanoreception" title="Mechanoreception">mechanoreceptor</a> cells (which detect motion on the animal's surface), and sometimes gland cells which secrete sticky fluids or even <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a>. The varied and often vivid colours of echinoderms are produced by the action of skin pigment cells. These are produced by a variable combination of coloured pigments, such as the dark <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a>, red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carotinoid" title="Carotinoid">carotinoids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carotene" title="Carotene">carotene</a> proteins, which can be blue, green, or violet. These may be light-sensitive, and as a result many echinoderms change appearance completely as night falls. The reaction can happen quickly — the sea urchin <i><a href="/wiki/Centrostephanus_longispinus" title="Centrostephanus longispinus">Centrostephanus longispinus</a></i> changes from jet black to grey-brown in just fifty minutes when exposed to light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>One characteristic of most echinoderms is a special kind of tissue known as "catch connective tissue". This collagenous material can change its mechanical properties in a few seconds or minutes through nervous control rather than by muscular means. This tissue enables a starfish to change from moving flexibly around the seabed to becoming rigid while prying open a <a href="/wiki/Bivalvia" title="Bivalvia">bivalve mollusc</a> or preventing itself from being extracted from a crevice. Similarly, sea urchins can lock their normally mobile spines rigidly as a defensive mechanism when attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_water_vascular_system">The water vascular system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The water vascular system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_vascular_system" title="Water vascular system">Water vascular system</a></div>
<p>Echinoderms possess a unique water vascular system. This is a network of fluid-filled canals derived from the <a href="/wiki/Coelom" title="Coelom">coelom</a> (body cavity) that function in gas exchange, feeding, sensory reception and locomotion. This system varies between different classes of echinoderm but typically opens to the exterior through a sieve-like <a href="/wiki/Madreporite" title="Madreporite">madreporite</a> on the aboral (upper) surface of the animal. The madreporite is linked to a slender duct, the stone canal, which extends to a ring canal that encircles the mouth or oesophagus. From this, radial canals extend along the arms of asteroids and adjoin the test in the ambulacral areas of echinoids. Short lateral canals branch off the radial canals, each one ending in an ampulla. Part of the ampulla can protrude through a pore (or a pair of pores in sea urchins) to the exterior and is known as a podium or <a href="/wiki/Tube_feet" title="Tube feet">tube feet</a>. The water vascular system assists with the distribution of nutrients throughout the animal's body and is most obviously expressed in the tube feet which can be extended or contracted by the redistribution of fluid between the foot and the internal sac.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit791_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit791-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The organization of the system is somewhat different in ophiuroids where the medreporite may be on the oral surface and the podia lack suckers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit785_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit785-24">[24]</a></sup> In holothuroids, the podia may be reduced or absent and the madreporite opens into the body cavity so that the circulating liquid is <a href="/wiki/Coelom#Coelomic_fluid" title="Coelom">coelomic fluid</a> rather than sea water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit789_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit789-25">[25]</a></sup> The arrangements in crinoids is similar to asteroids but the tube feet lack suckers and are used to pass food particles captured by the arms towards the central mouth. In the asteroids, the same wafting motion is employed to move the animal across the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit783_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit783-26">[26]</a></sup> Sea urchins use their feet to prevent the larvae of encrusting organisms from settling on their surfaces; potential settlers are moved to the urchin's mouth and eaten. Some burrowing sea stars extend their elongated dorsal tube feet to the surface of the sand or mud above and use them to absorb oxygen from the <a href="/wiki/Water_column" title="Water column">water column</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert905_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert905-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organs">Other organs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other organs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Echinoderms possess a simple digestive system which varies according to the animal's diet. Starfish are mostly carnivorous and have a mouth, oesophagus, two-part stomach, intestine and rectum, with the anus located in the centre of the aboral body surface. With a few exceptions, the members of the order <a href="/wiki/Paxillosida" title="Paxillosida">Paxillosida</a> do not possess an anus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In many species of starfish, the large cardiac stomach can be everted and digest food outside the body. In other species, whole food items such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a> may be ingested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert885_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert885-30">[30]</a></sup> Brittle stars have a blind gut with no intestine or anus. They have varying diets and expel food waste through their mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert891_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert891-31">[31]</a></sup> Sea urchins are herbivores and use their specialised mouthparts to graze, tear and chew <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and sometimes other animal or vegetable material. They have an oesophagus, a large stomach and a rectum with the anus at the apex of the test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert904_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert904-32">[32]</a></sup> Sea cucumbers are mostly <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>, sorting through the sediment with their buccal tentacles which are modified tube feet. Sand and mud accompanies their food through their simple gut which has a long coiled intestine and a capacious <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert912_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert912-33">[33]</a></sup> Crinoids are passive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suspension_feeder" title="Suspension feeder">suspension feeders</a>, catching <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> with their outstretched arms. Boluses of mucus-trapped food are passed to the mouth which is linked to the anus by a loop consisting of a short oesophagus and longer intestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert920_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert920-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Body_cavity" title="Body cavity">coelomic cavities</a> of echinoderms are complex. Aside from the water vascular system, echinoderms have a <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">haemal coelom</a> (or haemal system, the "haemal" being a misnomer), a perivisceral coelom, a gonadal coelom and often also a perihaemal coelom (or perihaemal system).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> During development, echinoderm coelom is divided in metacoel, mesocoel and protocoel (also called somatocoel, hydrocoel and axocoel, respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The water vascular system, haemal system and perihaemal system form the tubular coelomic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wbd.etibioinformatics.nl_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-wbd.etibioinformatics.nl-37">[37]</a></sup> Echinoderms are an exception having both a coelomic circulatory system (i.e., the water vascular system) and a haemal circulatory system (i.e., the haemal and perihaemal systems).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Haemal and perihaemal systems are derived from the coelom and form an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_circulatory_system" title="Open circulatory system">open</a> and reduced circulatory system. This usually consists of a central ring and five radial vessels. There is no true <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and the blood often lacks any respiratory pigment. Gaseous exchange occurs via dermal branchae or papulae in starfish, genital bursae in brittle stars, peristominal gills in sea urchins and cloacal trees in sea cucumbers. Exchange of gases also takes place through the tube feet. Echinoderms lack specialized excretory (waste disposal) organs and so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_waste" title="Nitrogenous waste">nitrogenous waste</a>, chiefly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, diffuses out through the respiratory surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit791_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit791-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The coelomic fluid contains the <a href="/wiki/Coelomocyte" title="Coelomocyte">coelomocytes</a>, or immune cells. There are several types of immune cells, which vary among classes and species. All classes possess a type of <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytic</a> amebocyte, which engulf invading particles and infected cells, aggregate or clot, and may be involved in <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxicity</a>. These cells are usually larger and granular, and are suggested to be a main line of defense against potential pathogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup> Depending on the class, echinoderms may have spherule cells (for cytotoxicity, inflammation, and anti-bacterial activity), vibratile cells (for coelomic fluid movement and clotting), and crystal cells (potential osmoregulatory cells in sea cucumbers),.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The coelomocytes also secrete <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_peptides" title="Antimicrobial peptides">Anti-Microbial Peptides</a> (AMPs) against bacteria, and have a set of <a href="/wiki/Lectin" title="Lectin">lectins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Complement_system" title="Complement system">complement proteins</a> as part of an innate immune system that is still being characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Echinoderms have a simple radial <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> that consists of a modified <a href="/wiki/Nerve_net" title="Nerve net">nerve net</a> consisting of interconnecting neurons with no central <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, although some do possess <a href="/wiki/Ganglion" title="Ganglion">ganglia</a>. Nerves radiate from central rings around the mouth into each arm or along the body wall; the branches of these nerves coordinate the movements of the organism and the synchronisation of the tube feet. Starfish have sensory cells in the epithelium and have simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocelli" title="Ocelli">eyespots</a> and touch-sensitive tentacle-like tube feet at the tips of their arms. Sea urchins have no particular sense organs but do have <a href="/wiki/Statocyst" title="Statocyst">statocysts</a> that assist in gravitational orientation, and they have sensory cells in their epidermis, particularly in the tube feet, spines and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedicellariae" title="Pedicellariae">pedicellariae</a>. Brittle stars, crinoids and sea cucumbers in general do not have sensory organs but some burrowing sea cucumbers of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <a href="/wiki/Apodida" title="Apodida">Apodida</a> have a single statocyst adjoining each radial nerve and some have an eyespot at the base of each tentacle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert929_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert929-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gonad" title="Gonad">gonads</a> occupy much of the body cavities of sea urchins and sea cucumbers, while the less voluminous crinoids, brittle stars and starfish have two gonads in each arm. While the ancestral condition is considered to be the possession of one genital aperture, many organisms have multiple <a href="/wiki/Gonopore" title="Gonopore">gonopores</a> through which eggs or sperm may be released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert929_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert929-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regeneration">Regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1274" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg/220px-Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg/330px-Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg/440px-Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_star_regenerating_legs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower_seastar" title="Sunflower seastar">Sunflower star</a> regenerating several arms</div></div></div>
<p>Many echinoderms have remarkable powers of <a href="/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)">regeneration</a>. Many species routinely <a href="/wiki/Autotomy" title="Autotomy">autotomize</a> and regenerate arms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscera" title="Viscera">viscera</a>. Sea cucumbers often discharge parts of their internal organs if they perceive themselves to be threatened. The discharged organs and tissues are regenerated over the course of several months. Sea urchins are constantly replacing spines lost through damage. Sea stars and sea lilies readily lose and regenerate their arms. In most cases, a single severed arm cannot grow into a new starfish in the absence of at least part of the disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmondson1935_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmondson1935-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAlaray1987_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-McAlaray1987-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotchkiss2000_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hotchkiss2000-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher1925_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher1925-45">[45]</a></sup> However, in a few species a single arm can survive and develop into a complete individual<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAlaray1987_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-McAlaray1987-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotchkiss2000_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hotchkiss2000-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher1925_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher1925-45">[45]</a></sup> and in some species, the arms are intentionally detached for the purpose of <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a>. During periods when they have lost their digestive tracts, sea cucumbers live off stored nutrients and absorb dissolved organic matter directly from the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dobson1991_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-dobson1991-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The regeneration of lost parts involves both <a href="/wiki/Epimorphosis" title="Epimorphosis">epimorphosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphallaxis" title="Morphallaxis">morphallaxis</a>. In epimorphosis stem cells—either from a reserve pool or those produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dedifferentiation" title="Dedifferentiation">dedifferentiation</a>—form a <a href="/wiki/Blastema" title="Blastema">blastema</a> and generate new tissues. Morphallactic regeneration involves the movement and remodelling of existing tissues to replace lost parts. Direct <a href="/wiki/Transdifferentiation" title="Transdifferentiation">transdifferentiation</a> of one type of tissue to another during tissue replacement is also observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mashanov2005_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mashanov2005-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The robust larval growth is responsible for many echinoderms being used as popular <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> in developmental biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_reproduction">Sexual reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sexual reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Echinoderms become sexually mature after approximately two to three years, depending on the species and the environmental conditions. They are nearly all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gonochoric" title="Gonochoric">gonochoric</a>, though a few species are <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphroditic</a>. The eggs and sperm cells are typically released into open water, where fertilization takes place. The release of sperm and eggs is synchronised in some species, usually with regard to the lunar cycle. In other species, individuals may aggregate during the reproductive season, thereby increasing the likelihood of successful fertilisation. Internal fertilisation has currently been observed in three species of sea star, three brittle stars and a deep water sea cucumber. Even at abyssal depths, where no light penetrates, synchronisation of reproductive activity in echinoderms is surprisingly frequent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some echinoderms brood their eggs. This is especially common in cold water species where planktonic larvae might not be able to find sufficient food. These retained eggs are usually few in number and are supplied with large yolks to nourish the developing embryos. In starfish, the female may carry the eggs in special pouches, under her arms, under her arched body or even in her cardiac stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert887-8_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert887-8-50">[50]</a></sup> Many brittle stars are hermaphrodites. Egg brooding is quite common and usually takes place in special chambers on their oral surfaces, but sometimes the ovary or coelom is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert895_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert895-51">[51]</a></sup> In these starfish and brittle stars, direct development without passing through a bilateral larval stage usually takes place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert888_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert888-52">[52]</a></sup> A few sea urchins and one species of sand dollar carry their eggs in cavities, or near their anus, holding them in place with their spines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert908_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert908-53">[53]</a></sup> Some sea cucumbers use their buccal tentacles to transfer their eggs to their underside or back where they are retained. In a very small number of species, the eggs are retained in the coelom where they develop viviparously, later emerging through ruptures in the body wall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert916_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert916-54">[54]</a></sup> In some species of crinoid, the embryos develop in special breeding bags, where the eggs are held until sperm released by a male happens to find them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert922_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert922-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asexual_reproduction">Asexual reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Asexual reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seastar" title="Seastar">seastar</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ophidiaster_granifer" title="Ophidiaster granifer">Ophidiaster granifer</a></i>, reproduces asexually by <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">parthenogenesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamaguchi1984_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yamaguchi1984-56">[56]</a></sup>  In certain other <a href="/wiki/Asterozoa" title="Asterozoa">asterozoans</a>, the adults reproduce asexually for a while before they mature after which time they reproduce sexually. In most of these species, asexual reproduction is by transverse fission with the disc splitting in two. Regrowth of both the lost disc area and the missing arms occur<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher1925_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher1925-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> so that an individual may have arms of varying lengths. Though in most species at least part of the disc is needed for complete regeneration, in a few species of sea stars, a single severed arm can grow into a complete individual over a period of several months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAlaray1987_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-McAlaray1987-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotchkiss2000_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hotchkiss2000-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher1925_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher1925-45">[45]</a></sup> In at least some of these species, they actively use this as a method of asexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAlaray1987_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-McAlaray1987-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monks1904_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monks1904-58">[58]</a></sup> A fracture develops on the lower surface of the arm and the arm pulls itself free from the body which holds onto the substrate during the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monks1904_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monks1904-58">[58]</a></sup> During the period of regrowth, they have a few tiny arms and one large arm, thus often being referred to as "comets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hotchkiss2000_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hotchkiss2000-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monks1904_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monks1904-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Asexual reproduction by transverse <a href="/wiki/Fission_(biology)" title="Fission (biology)">fission</a> has also been observed in adult sea cucumbers. <i><a href="/wiki/Holothuria_parvula" title="Holothuria parvula">Holothuria parvula</a></i> uses this method frequently, an individual splitting into two a little in front of the midpoint. The two halves each regenerate their missing organs over a period of several months but the missing genital organs are often very slow to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kille1942_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kille1942-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The larvae of some echinoderm species are capable of asexual reproduction. This has long been known to occur among starfish and brittle stars but has been more recently observed in a sea cucumber, a sand dollar and a sea urchin. These species belong to four of the major classes of echinoderms except <a href="/wiki/Crinozoa" title="Crinozoa">crinozoans</a> (as of 2011).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eaves2003_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eaves2003-60">[60]</a></sup> Asexual reproduction in the planktonic larvae occurs through numerous modes. They may <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction)" title="Fragmentation (reproduction)">autotomise</a> parts that develop into secondary larvae, grow <a href="/wiki/Budding" title="Budding">buds</a> or undergo <a href="/wiki/Paratomy" title="Paratomy">paratomy</a>. The parts that are autotomised or the buds may develop directly into fully formed larvae or may develop through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastrula" title="Gastrula">gastrula</a> or even a <a href="/wiki/Blastula" title="Blastula">blastula</a> stage. The parts that develop into the new larvae vary from the preoral hood (a mound like structure above the mouth), the side body wall, the postero-lateral arms or their rear ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eaves2003_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eaves2003-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaeckle1994_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaeckle1994-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2009_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2009-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The process of cloning is a cost borne by the larva both in resources as well as in development time. Larvae have been observed to undergo this process when food is plentiful<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald2010_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald2010-63">[63]</a></sup> or temperature conditions are optimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2009_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2009-62">[62]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that cloning may occur to make use of the tissues that are normally lost during metamorphosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2008_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2008-64">[64]</a></sup> Recent research has shown that the larvae of some sand dollars clone themselves when they detect predators (by sensing dissolved fish mucus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2009_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2009-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2008_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2008-64">[64]</a></sup> Asexual reproduction produces many smaller larvae that escape better from planktivorous fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughn2010_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughn2010-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Larval_development">Larval development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Larval development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pluteus001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="156" data-file-width="150" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/Pluteus001.jpg" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pluteus001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An echinopluteus larva with larval arms</div></div></div>
<p>The development of an echinoderm begins with a bilaterally symmetrical embryo, with a coeloblastula developing first. <a href="/wiki/Gastrulation" title="Gastrulation">Gastrulation</a> marks the opening of the "second mouth" that places echinoderms within the deuterostomes, and the mesoderm, which will host the skeleton, migrates inwards. The secondary body cavity, the coelom, forms by the partitioning of three body cavities. The larvae are mostly planktonic but in some species the eggs are retained inside the female and in some, the larvae are also brooded by the female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doritlarvae_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doritlarvae-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The larvae of echinoderms pass through a number of stages and these have specific names derived from the taxonomic names of the adults or from their appearance. For example, a sea urchin has an 'echinopluteus' larva while a brittle star has an 'ophiopluteus' larva. A starfish has a 'bipinnaria' larva but this later develops into a multi-armed 'brachiolaria' larva. A sea cucumber larva is an 'auricularia' while a crinoid one is a 'vitellaria'. All these larvae are bilaterally symmetrical and have bands of cilia with which they swim and some, usually known as 'pluteus' larvae, have arms. When fully developed they settle on the seabed to undergo metamorphosis and the larval arms and gut degenerate. The left hand side of the larva develops into the oral surface of the juvenile while the right side becomes the aboral surface. At this stage the bilateral symmetry is lost and radial symmetry develops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doritlarvae_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doritlarvae-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planktotrophic" title="Planktotrophic">planktotrophic</a> larva is considered to be the ancestral larval type for echinoderms but after 500 million years of larval evolution, about 68% of species whose development is known have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lecithotrophic" title="Lecithotrophic">lecithotrophic</a> larval type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uthicke_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Uthicke-10">[10]</a></sup> The provision of a yolk-sac means that smaller numbers of eggs are produced, the larvae have a shorter development period, smaller dispersal potential but a greater chance of survival. There seems to be an evolutionary trend towards a "lower-risk–lower-gain" strategy of direct development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uthicke_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Uthicke-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Echinoderms are globally distributed in almost all depths, latitudes and environments in the ocean. They reach highest diversity in reef environments but are also widespread on shallow shores, around the poles — refugia where crinoids are at their most abundant — and throughout the deep ocean, where bottom-dwelling and burrowing sea cucumbers are common — sometimes accounting for up to 90% of organisms. While almost all echinoderms are benthic — that is, they live on the sea floor — some sea-lilies can swim at great velocity for brief periods of time, and a few deep-sea sea cucumbers are fully floating. Some crinoids are pseudo-planktonic, attaching themselves to floating logs and debris, although this behaviour was exercised most extensively in the Paleozoic, before competition from such organisms as barnacles restricted the extent of the behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The larvae of echinoderms, especially starfish and sea urchins, are pelagic, and with the aid of ocean currents can be transported for great distances, reinforcing the global distribution of the phylum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mode_of_life">Mode of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mode of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Locomotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Echinoderms primarily use their tube feet to move about but some sea urchins also use their spines. The tube feet typically have a tip shaped like a suction pad in which a vacuum can be created by contraction of muscles. This along with some stickiness provided by the secretion of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> provides adhesion. Waves of tube feet contractions and relaxations move along the adherent surface and the animal moves slowly along.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Brittle stars are the most agile of the echinoderms, raising their discs and taking strides when moving. The two forward arms grip the substrate with their tube feet, the two side arms "row", the hindermost arm trails and the animal moves in jerks. The arm spines provide traction and when moving among objects, the supple arms can coil around things. A few species creep around on pointed tube feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-70">[70]</a></sup> Starfish extend their tube feet in the intended direction of travel and grip the substrate by suction, after which the feet are drawn backwards. The movement of multiple tube feet, coordinated in waves, moves the animal forward, but progress is slow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Some burrowing starfish have points rather than suckers on their tube feet and they are able to "glide" across the seabed at a faster rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea urchins use their tube feet to move around in a similar way to starfish. Some also use their articulated spines to push or lever themselves along or lift their oral surfaces off the substrate. If a sea urchin is overturned, it can extend its tube feet in one ambulacral area far enough to bring them within reach of the substrate and then successively attach feet from the adjoining area until it is righted. Some species bore into rock and they usually do this by grinding away at the surface with their mouthparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert900_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert900-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea cucumbers are generally sluggish animals. Many can move on the surface or burrow through sand or mud using <a href="/wiki/Peristalsis" title="Peristalsis">peristaltic</a> movements and some have short tube feet on their under surface with which they can creep along in the manner of a starfish. Some species drag themselves along by means of their buccal tentacles while others can expand and contract their body or rhythmically flex it and "swim". Many live in cracks, hollows and burrows and hardly move at all. Some deep water species are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> and can float in the water with webbed papillae forming sails or fins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert911-2_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert911-2-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The majority of crinoids are motile but the sea lilies are sessile and attached to hard substrates by stalks. These stems can bend and the arms can roll and unroll and that is about the limit of the sea lily's movement, although a few species can relocate themselves on the seabed by crawling. The sea feathers are unattached and usually live in crevices, under corals or inside sponges with their arms the only visible part. Some sea feathers emerge at night and perch themselves on nearby eminences to better exploit the food-bearing current. Many species can "walk" across the seabed, raising their body with the help of their arms. Many can also swim with their arms but most are largely sedentary, seldom moving far from their chosen place of concealment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feeding_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feeding-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The modes of feeding vary greatly between the different echinoderm taxa. Crinoids and some brittle stars tend to be passive filter-feeders, enmeshing suspended particles from passing water; most sea urchins are grazers, sea cucumbers deposit feeders and the majority of starfish are active hunters.
</p><p>Crinoids are suspension feeders and spread their arms wide to catch particles floating past. These are caught by the tube feet on the pinnules, moved into the ambulacral grooves, wrapped in mucus and conveyed to the mouth by the cilia lining the grooves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The exact dietary requirements of crinoids have been little researched but in the laboratory they can be fed with diatoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Basket stars are suspension feeders, raising their branched arms to collect <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, while brittle stars use several methods of feeding, though usually one predominates. Some are suspension feeders, securing food particles with mucus strands, spines or tube feet on their raised arms. Others are scavengers and feeders on detritus. Others again are voracious carnivores and able to lasso their waterborne prey with a sudden encirclement by their flexible arms. The limbs then bend under the disc to transfer the food to the jaws and mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert893_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert893-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many sea urchins feed on algae, often scraping off the thin layer of algae covering the surfaces of rocks with their specialised mouthparts known as Aristotle's lantern. Other species devour smaller organisms, which they may catch with their tube feet. They may also feed on dead fish and other animal matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Sand dollars may perform suspension feeding and feed on <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, detritus, algal pieces and the bacterial layer surrounding grains of sand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many sea cucumbers are mobile deposit or suspension feeders, using their buccal podia to actively capture food and then stuffing the particles individually into their buccal cavities. Others ingest large quantities of sediment, absorb the organic matter and pass the indigestible mineral particles through their guts. In this way they disturb and process large volumes of substrate, often leaving characteristic ridges of sediment on the seabed. Some sea cucumbers live infaunally in burrows, anterior-end down and anus on the surface, swallowing sediment and passing it through their gut. Other burrowers live anterior-end up and wait for detritus to fall into the entrances of the burrows or rake in debris from the surface nearby with their buccal podia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert914_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert914-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nearly all starfish are detritivores or carnivores, though a few are suspension feeders. Small fish landing on the upper surface may be captured by pedicilaria and dead animal matter may be scavenged but the main prey items are living invertebrates, mostly bivalve molluscs. To feed on one of these, the starfish moves over it, attaches its tube feet and exerts pressure on the valves by arching its back. When a small gap between the valves is formed, the starfish inserts part of its stomach into the prey, excretes digestive <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> and slowly liquefies the soft body parts. As the adductor muscle of the shellfish relaxes, more stomach is inserted and when digestion is complete, the stomach is returned to its usual position in the starfish with its now liquefied bivalve meal inside it. The same everted stomach process is used by other starfish to feed on sponges, sea anemones, corals, detritus and algal films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruppert884-5_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruppert884-5-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defense_mechanisms">Defense mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Defense mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Despite their low nutrition value and the abundance of indigestible calcite, echinoderms are the prey of many organisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, sea birds and other echinoderms. Defensive strategies employed include the presence of spines, toxins, which can be inherent or delivered through the tube feet, and the discharge of sticky entangling threads by sea cucumbers. Although most echinoderm spines are blunt, those of the crown-of-thorns starfish are long and sharp and can cause a painful puncture wound as the epithelium covering them contains a toxin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit779_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit779-6">[6]</a></sup> Because of their catch connective tissue, which can change rapidly from a flaccid to a rigid state, echinoderms are very difficult to dislodge from crevices. Certain sea cucumbers have a cluster of <a href="/wiki/Cuvierian_tubules" title="Cuvierian tubules">cuvierian tubules</a> which can be ejected as long sticky threads from their anus and entangle and permanently disable an attacker. Another defensive strategy sometimes adopted by sea cucumbers is to rupture the body wall and discharge the gut and internal organs. The animal has a great regenerative capacity and will regrow the lost parts later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorit790_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorit790-83">[83]</a></sup> Starfish and brittle stars may undergo autotomy when attacked, an arm becoming detached which may distract the predator for long enough for the animal to escape. Some starfish species can "swim" away from what may be danger, foregoing the regrowth by not losing limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mladenov1989_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mladenov1989-84">[84]</a></sup> It is not unusual to find starfish with arms of different sizes in various stages of regrowth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Echinoderms are numerous and relatively large invertebrates and play an important role in marine, benthic <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uthicke_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Uthicke-10">[10]</a></sup> The grazing of sea urchins reduces the rate of colonization of bare rock by settling organisms but also keeps algae in check, thereby enhancing the biodiversity of coral reefs. The burrowing of <a href="/wiki/Echinarachnius_parma" title="Echinarachnius parma">sand dollars</a>, sea cucumbers and some starfish stirs up the sediment and depletes the sea floor of nutrients. Their digging activities increases the depth to which oxygen can seep and allows a more complex ecological tier-system to develop. Starfish and brittle stars prevent the growth of algal mats on coral reefs, which might otherwise obstruct the filter-feeding constituent organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-86">[86]</a></sup> Some sea urchins can bore into solid rock and this <a href="/wiki/Bioerosion" title="Bioerosion">bioerosion</a> can destabilise rock faces and release nutrients into the ocean. Coral reefs are also bored into in this way but the rate of accretion of carbonate material is often greater than the erosion produced by the sea urchin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> It has been estimated that echinoderms capture and sequester about 0.1 gigatonnes of carbon per year as calcium carbonate, making them important contributors in the global carbon cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Echinoderms sometimes have large population swings which can cause marked consequences for ecosystems. An example is the change from a coral-dominated reef system to an alga-dominated one that resulted from the mass mortality of the tropical sea urchin <i><a href="/wiki/Diadema_antillarum" title="Diadema antillarum">Diadema antillarum</a></i> in the Caribbean in 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Sea urchins are among the main herbivores on reefs and there is usually a fine balance between the urchins and the kelp and other algae on which they graze. A diminution of the numbers of predators (otters, lobsters and fish) can result in an increase in urchin numbers causing overgrazing of <a href="/wiki/Kelp_forest" title="Kelp forest">kelp forests</a> with the result that an alga-denuded "<a href="/wiki/Urchin_barren" title="Urchin barren">urchin barren</a>" forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>, an unexplained increase in the numbers of <a href="/wiki/Crown-of-thorns_starfish" title="Crown-of-thorns starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a> (<i>Acanthaster planci</i>), which graze on living coral tissue, has had considerable impact on coral mortality and coral reef <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Echinoderms form part of the diet of many organisms such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eider_duck" title="Eider duck">eider ducks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastropoda" title="Gastropoda">gastropod molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">sea otters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic foxes</a> and humans. Larger starfish prey on smaller ones and the great quantity of eggs and larvae produced form part of the <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, consumed by many marine creatures. Crinoids are relatively free from predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-86">[86]</a></sup> The body cavities of many sea cucumbers and some starfish provide a habitat for parasitic or symbiotic organisms including fish, crabs, worms and snails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_by_humans">Use by humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Echinoderm&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Use by humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2010, 373,000 tonnes of echinoderms were harvested, mainly for consumption. These were mainly sea cucumbers (158,000 tonnes) and sea urchins (73,000 tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Sea cucumbers are considered a delicacy in some countries of south east Asia; as such, they are in imminent danger of being over-harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Popular species include the pineapple roller <i><a href="/wiki/Thelenota_ananas" title="Thelenota ananas">Thelenota ananas</a></i> (<i>susuhan</i>) and the red <i><a href="/wiki/Holothuria_edulis" title="Holothuria edulis">Holothuria edulis</a></i>.  These and other species are colloquially known as <i>bêche de mer</i> or <i>trepang</i> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. The sea cucumbers are boiled for twenty minutes and then dried both naturally and later over a fire which gives them a smoky tang. In China they are used as a basis for gelatinous soups and stews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Both male and female gonads of sea urchins are also consumed particularly in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. The taste is described as soft and melting, like a mixture of seafood and fruit. The quality is assessed by the colour which can range from light yellow to bright orange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> At the present time, some trials of breeding sea uchins in order to try to compensate the overexploitation of this resource have been made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>The calcareous tests or shells of echinoderms are used as a source of lime by farmers in areas where limestone is unavailable and some are used in the manufacture of fish meal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Four thousand tons of the animals are used annually for these purposes. This trade is often carried out in conjunction with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">shellfish</a> farmers, for whom the starfish pose a major threat by eating their cultured stock. Other uses for the starfish they recover include the manufacture of animal feed, composting and drying for the arts and craft trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barkhouse_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barkhouse-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea urchins are used in research, particularly as model organisms in <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insight_from_the_Sea_Urchin_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Insight_from_the_Sea_Urchin-100">[100]</a></sup> and ecotoxicology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Strongylocentrotus_purpuratus" title="Strongylocentrotus purpuratus">Strongylocentrotus purpuratus</a></i>  and <i><a href="/wiki/Arbacia_punctulata" title="Arbacia punctulata">Arbacia punctulata</a></i> are used for this purpose in embryological studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The large size and the transparency of the eggs enables the observation of <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> cells in the process of fertilising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">ova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insight_from_the_Sea_Urchin_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Insight_from_the_Sea_Urchin-100">[100]</a></sup> The arm regeneration potential of brittle stars is being studied in connection with understanding and treating <a href="/wiki/Neurodegeneration" title="Neurodegeneration">neurodegenerative</a> diseases in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barkhouse_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barkhouse-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
